                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/aenm.202203802')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d6c33c23b25143559bae191b3af3939e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c1476327-5ac5-47b4-bd21-8b4555340a4e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d32229221" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries owing to its excellent cycling stability and low cost. However, insufficient sodium storage sites still hinder its practical applications. Herein, a facile strategy to induce the efficient structural transformation from the tunnel to the layered type of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> by trace W-doping for the first time is reported. The W-doping not only enriches the sodium storage sites but also improves the cycling performance. As a result, the phase-pure P2-Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>W<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> demonstrates an enhanced reversible specific capacity of 195.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and energy density of 517&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C, accompanying superior cycling stability with capacity retention of 80% over 200 cycles. Moreover, the W-doped samples show high structure stability in a moist atmosphere and can still maintain the original electrochemical performance after water treatment. In situ and ex situ characterizations reveal the enhanced structural stability of the P2-Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>W<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrodes. This work provides a facile strategy on the structural engineering of transition metal oxides to induce the tunnel-to-layered structure transformation and could shed light on the design and construction of stable and high-capacity cathode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202203802-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) dominate the portable electronics and electric vehicle markets owing to their high energy density. However, with the continuous consumption of lithium resources, the situation of lithium resource scarcity is gradually formed, thus promoting the battery community to seek alternative electrochemical energy storage technologies. Compared with LIBs, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have the advantages of wide distribution, high abundance and low cost of Na resources, which endow them with a vital role in grid energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0001_R_d32229211e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Electrochem. Energ. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0003_R_d32229211e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - eScience">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon is currently considered the most promising anode material for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0004_R_d32229211e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Carbon Energy">4</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0007_R_d32229211e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Mater. Adv.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the cathode materials suitable for practical application are still under controversy. A wide range of cathode materials have been investigated, including transition metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0008_R_d32229211e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0011_R_d32229211e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Mater. Adv.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanion compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0012_R_d32229211e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0016_R_d32229211e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Mater. Adv.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blues analogues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0017_R_d32229211e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Sci.">17</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0020_R_d32229211e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the layered transition metal oxides (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TmO<sub>2</sub>, with Tm&nbsp;=&nbsp;Mn, Ni, Co, Cr, Fe, etc.) have attracted much more interest for their facile synthesis, diverse composition, high theoretical specific capacities, and reliable electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0021_R_d32229211e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - eScience">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the transition metal oxides, P2-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnO<sub>2</sub> stands in the spotlight owing to its low cost, environmental sustainability, and decent electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0022_R_d32229211e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the structure of the P2 type system is more stable during Na ion insertion/removal compared to the O3 counterpart.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0023_R_d32229211e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, the P2-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnO<sub>2</sub> compounds are still haunted by irreversible structural changes/phase transitions during cycling, insufficient electrochemical performances, and air/moisture instability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0024_R_d32229211e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Acc. Chem. Res.">24</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0026_R_d32229211e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Besides, there is a variety of tunnel oxide materials represented by Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, whose structure is very stable and can even work in the aqueous electrolytes, accompanied by remarkable cycling stability as cathode materials for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0027_R_d32229211e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0028_R_d32229211e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the primary problem that restricts the development of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is its low capacity (theoretical capacity is 121&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) due to limited Na storage sites. Dai et&nbsp;al. proposed that the Na content of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> varies between 0.22 and 0.66 during charge and discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0029_R_d32229211e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Xiao et&nbsp;al. found that when the content of Na gradually increased to ≈0.6 during the synthesis, composite materials with layered and tunnel structures were obtained, delivering a high reversible capacity of 198.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0030_R_d32229211e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ion doping (e.g., Al/F,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0031_R_d32229211e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mg,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0032_R_d32229211e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Sci.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Co,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0033_R_d32229211e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) is also an effective strategy to improve the electrochemical performance of tunnel Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, but the doping amount is usually excess and the performance enhancement is limited. Tunnel-structured metal oxides and layered metal oxides have different crystal structures, which determine their electrochemistry properties. How to construct Mn-based host structures to integrate the advantages of the high capacity from layered materials and high stability from tunnel materials remains a great challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report the introduction of high-valence tungsten (W) ions into Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> to efficiently induce the conversion of the tunnel structure to the layered structure for the first time. Compared with the reported doped metal ions, W can induce the phase transformation of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> at a pretty low doping concentration. With trace doping of 1%, tunnel Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is transformed into layered P2-Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>W<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMO-1 W). The NMO-1&nbsp;W demonstrates a high reversible specific capacity of 195.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a remarkable energy density of 517&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which is much higher than the Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (110&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 331&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>). Moreover, the NMO-1&nbsp;W exhibits superior cycling stability with high capacity retention of 80% for 200 cycles. Besides, due to the improved water resistance by W-doping, the NMO-1&nbsp;W after water treatment maintains the original electrochemical performance. In situ and ex situ characterizations confirm the improved structure stability of the W-doped samples. This work proposes a low-concentration doping method to initiate the tunnel-to-layered phase transition of transition metal oxides efficiently and provides a novel cathode material for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202203802-sec-0020-title">2 Results and discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The atomic ratios of synthesized material were measured by inductively coupled plasma emission (ICP) spectrometry, which is in good agreement with the theoretical values (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). To investigate the effect of W-doping on the crystal structure evolution, X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were measured from 10° to 70° for the different samples (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1a</a>). The diffraction patterns of these samples reveal the gradual structure transformation from tunnel structure (T-type) to layered structure (P2-type) by W-doping. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1b</a> shows that pristine Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> material (NMO-0&nbsp;W) has a typical orthorhombic structure with the <i>Pbam</i> space group (JCPDS no. 27–0750; Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). In detail, MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedrons and MnO<sub>5</sub> square pyramids form the framework of the tunnel structure, in which Mn has five different types of occupation sites. Among them, the Mn5 site is considered to be hinged to relieve lattice strains and changes during the insertion/extraction of sodium ions. The Mn1 Mn3 and Mn4 sites are occupied by Mn<sup>4+</sup>, while the Mn2 and Mn5 sites are occupied by Mn<sup>3+</sup>. Two kinds of tunnels appear in the framework of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The large S-shaped tunnel is half full of Na (Na2 and Na3), while another small tunnel site is full of Na (Na1). The latter Na cannot be reversibly extracted/inserted. Some studies have found that the Na2 site can be further divided into two sub-sites with slight dislocations but completely different environments, and Na in this part is involved in the charging and discharging process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0034_R_d32229211e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0035_R_d32229211e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After introducing the W element into Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, a grand new strong diffraction peak appears at ≈15.9°, which can be well indexed to P2-type layered structure with a space group of <i>P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc</i> (JCPDS no. 27–0751), indicating the occurrence of phase transformation from T-type to the P2-type layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0030_R_d32229211e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When doped with an extremely low concentration of W of 0.5% (Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.995</sub>W<sub>0.005</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, NMO-0.5&nbsp;W), the sample exhibits very significant phase transformation. The main Bragg diffraction of NMO-0.5&nbsp;W shows a mixture of P2-type and T-type structures. According to the XRD Rietveld refinement results, the percentages of the layered and tunnel phases are 80% and 20%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). With further increasing the W content to 1%, the diffraction peaks of the tunnel phase completely disappear, and all the peaks of NMO-1&nbsp;W can be well-ascribed to the P2 phase with space group <i>P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc</i>, Mn and W atoms are settled on the <i>2a</i> site of the transition-metal layer (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The major peak of the tunnel phase near 34° only can be observed in NMO-0&nbsp;W and NMO-0.5&nbsp;W (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1c</a>), indicating that the sample can completely transform from the original tunnel structure to pure layered structure when the doping concentration of W increases to 1%. The main peak of the tunnel phase weakens first and then completely disappears with the increase of W-doping, indicating that it enables the promotion of the transformation of the tunnel phase into the P2 phase. The Rietveld refinement results and structure model diagram of the layered structure are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1d</a>. For sodium manganese oxide (Ma<i><sub>x</sub></i>MnO<sub>2</sub>), when the <i>x</i>&lt;0.5, it usually shows tunnel-type oxide, while it exhibits a layered-type oxide when the content of sodium is increased to <i>x</i>&gt;0.5.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0036_R_d32229211e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the NMO-1&nbsp;W with a low Na content of 0.44 demonstrates the typical P2-layered structure, in which Mn and O form octahedral coordination (MnO<sub>6</sub>). The transition metal slabs (MnO<sub>2</sub>) are built by the edge-sharing between the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedral, in which sodium ions are accommodated. Therefore, the structure of alternating MnO<sub>2</sub> slabs and Na layer is formed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0037_R_d32229211e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the content of W increases to 2% (Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.98</sub>W<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, NMO-2&nbsp;W), an apparent amount of impurity phase exists which can be identified as Na<sub>2</sub>WO<sub>4</sub> (space group: <i>Fd-3m</i>, JCPDS no. 12–0772). It implies that the doping concentration of 2% may be excessive for the Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> to completely accept, therefore the rest of the W will separately exist in NMO-2&nbsp;W as impurities. Except for the impurity peaks, the diffraction peak from the tunnel structure is still not observed. So NMO-2&nbsp;W is composed of layered material and impurities (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). To further investigate the effect of W on the NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, NMO-1&nbsp;W, and NMO-2&nbsp;W, the region of 15–16.5° was amplified in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1e</a>. The diffraction peak in this area is indexed to the (002) crystal plane of the P2-type structure. After introducing W into the sample, the (002) peak gradually shifts to the lower degree with the increase of the doping concentration, indicating that W has successfully been doped into the lattice of the layered structure and the lattice spacing related to (002) crystal plane is expanded. In contrast, the diffraction peak (100) at ≈36.2° moves to a higher angle with the increase of doping concentration, representing the contraction of the crystal <i>ab</i> plane. The lattice parameter <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> were obtained from the Rietveld refinement results of all the W-doped samples (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S5</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1f</a> exhibits the evolution of the lattice parameters. It can be seen that lattice parameter <i>c</i> expends from 11.316&nbsp;Å (NMO-0.5&nbsp;W) to 11.338&nbsp;Å (NMO-2&nbsp;W) as the W concentration increases, and this result coincides well with the <i>d</i> value. It may be due to that the W<sup>6+</sup> enters the transition metal layer and has a strong repulsion with Na<sup>+</sup> because of the positive hexavalent state. For the sake of maintaining charge conservation, the concentration of sodium ions around tungsten is pretty low. Furthermore, the bond energy of W<span class="icomoon"></span>O (672&nbsp;KJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>) is much stronger than the bond energy of Mn<span class="icomoon"></span>O (402&nbsp;KJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>), which weakens the force between Na and O. Therefore, the reduction of Na content and Na-O force in the sodium layer results in the slight expansion of the layer distance. Lattice parameter <i>a</i>/<i>b</i> (<i>a</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;<i>b</i> for <i>P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc</i>) shrinks when introducing more W into samples. That can be explained by the high concentration of W in MnO<sub>2</sub> slabs and the strong energy bond of W-O, leading to further shrinkage of the transition metal layer in the direction of the <i>ab</i> plane.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202203802-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/78da3c30-ca65-4ee0-9199-a2815b216f58/aenm202203802-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/78da3c30-ca65-4ee0-9199-a2815b216f58/aenm202203802-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ea30059-1c92-4c12-b9eb-451e05fb7a13/aenm202203802-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/78da3c30-ca65-4ee0-9199-a2815b216f58/aenm202203802-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202203802-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202203802" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of NMO-0&nbsp;W, NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, NMO-1&nbsp;W, and NMO-2&nbsp;W samples. a) X-ray diffraction patterns. b) Rietveld refinements of NMO-0&nbsp;W and corresponding crystal structure. c) Enlarged X-ray diffraction patterns from 30° to 36°. d) Rietveld refinements and crystal structure of NMO-1&nbsp;W. e) Enlarged X-ray diffraction patterns from 15.0° to 16.5°. f) Changes in lattice parameters of different samples. The XPS survey spectra for g) W 4f and h) Mn 2p.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To verify the evolution in the oxidation states of Mn and W, high-resolution X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra were performed for all samples. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1g</a> confirms the presence of W elements in as-prepared samples. Two distinct peaks can be assigned to W 4f<sub>7/2</sub> (≈35&nbsp;eV) and W 4f<sub>5/2</sub> (≈37&nbsp;eV) of hexavalent W ions, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0038_R_d32229211e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1h</a>, all Mn 2p is composed of two broad characteristic peaks of 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> centered at ≈642 and 653&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0039_R_d32229211e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, each peak can be divided into two distinct peaks, which are ascribed to the Mn<sup>3+</sup> at ≈642 and 653&nbsp;eV and Mn<sup>4+</sup> at ≈643 and 654&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating the existence of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> in all the samples. Nevertheless, it is worth pointing out that the semiquantitative ratio of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> increases from 0.84 to 0.89 after introducing W (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). What's more, the characteristic peaks of Mn 2p shift to higher binding energy positions, which is closely related to the electron density. When Mn is replaced by W, the randomly distributed transition metal ions enable pretty strong interactions, resulting in different electron densities in the outer layer of Mn ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0040_R_d32229211e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Eng. J.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0002">2a–c</a> exhibits the morphologies of NMO-0&nbsp;W, NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, and NMO-1&nbsp;W. Notably, W-doping has a significant influence on the morphologies of the samples. Typical rod-like particles with a width of ≈1–2&nbsp;µm and a length between 2–4&nbsp;µm of tunnel structure can be observed for the NMO-0&nbsp;W (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0002">2a</a>). As for NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, it consists of plate-like particles along with rod-like particles, indicating a mixture of tunnel structure and layered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0002">2b</a>). This observation is well consistent with the XRD results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0001">1a</a>). Besides, two different particles exist independently without any interconnection in structure. And it is found that the pristine NMO-0&nbsp;W is very fluffy, while the W-substituted sample becomes dense for the powder samples. This results from the phase transition along with the morphology changes from rod structure to plate shape, which is packed more tightly. As demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0002">2c</a>, only hexagonal plate-like morphology with a regular shape can be found for the NMO-1&nbsp;W. The size of the particles is ≈1–2&nbsp;µm in width with a thickness of ≈0.5-1&nbsp;µm. For the NMO-2&nbsp;W, although the morphology is still a hexagonal plate-like structure with increasing content of W-doping, distinct irregular morphology can be seen around the plate-like particles, which may be the Na<sub>2</sub>WO<sub>4</sub> impurity (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The EDS mapping results of rod-like particles and plate-like particles in NMO-0.5&nbsp;W are provided to show the element distribution. It can be concluded that the sodium, manganese, and oxygen are uniformly distributed in both tunnel and layered structures. However, W cannot be detected in the rod-like structure, while little W can be examined in the plate-like structure, further confirming that the W cannot be doped in the tunnel structure and the introduction of W induces the phase transformation. In other words, W can only substitute the Mn sites of the P2 phase, which may efficiently stabilize the layered structure even with a low Na content. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) analysis suggests clear lattice fringes of NMO-0.5&nbsp;W as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0002">2f,i</a>. The d-spacing of 0.458&nbsp;nm in the rod belongs to the interplanar distance of the (200) plane of the tunnel structure, while the lattice spacing in the plate is determined to be 0.285&nbsp;nm, which could be ascribed to the (100) plane of the P2 layered structure Further characterizations confirm that that W is uniformly distributed on the plates, while W cannot be detected on the rods (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5–8</a>, Supporting Information). We further carried out ex situ XRD to investigate the specific influence of W on the structural evolution during the sintering process (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The results indicate that W can efficiently stabilize the structure to maintain the layered phase during the whole sintering. And density functional theory (DFT) calculations showed that the formation energy of tunnel structure is higher than that of the layered structure with W introduced in the structure, indicating that the latter is more stable (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202203802-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d1c9cb82-7b5c-4520-af56-2e6f20903af8/aenm202203802-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d1c9cb82-7b5c-4520-af56-2e6f20903af8/aenm202203802-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e710cd22-13b1-4884-9e23-d17f9ce70853/aenm202203802-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d1c9cb82-7b5c-4520-af56-2e6f20903af8/aenm202203802-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202203802-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202203802" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of prepared samples for a) NMO-0&nbsp;W, b) NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, and c) NMO-1&nbsp;W. d,g) Typical TEM images and corresponding e,h) EDS elemental mappings, and f,i) HRTEM images (insets are the SADP) for (d-f) the rod-like structure and g–i) the plate-like structure of NMO-0.5&nbsp;W.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Sodium storage properties of the obtained samples were investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3a</a> shows typical CV curves of NMO-0&nbsp;W and NMO-1&nbsp;W electrodes at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. Six pairs of very symmetrical redox peaks can be observed in the range of 2.0-4.0&nbsp;V for the NMO-0&nbsp;W electrode, indicating that the tunnel NMO-0&nbsp;W undergoes a complex multiphase transformation during the Na-ion intercalation and extraction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0035_R_d32229211e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the CV curves of the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode, a series of complex redox peaks also occur, exhibiting high reversible oxidation-reduction of the P2-type structure. According to the redox and phase transition mechanisms,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0041_R_d32229211e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">41</a>-<a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0043_R_d32229211e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mater. Chem. A">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the CV curves of the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode can be roughly separated into two regions. In the range of 2.5-4.0&nbsp;V, the redox peaks correspond to the charge ordering or Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy order-disorder transition in layered materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0044_R_d32229211e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different from the stable tunnel structure, the layered structure of P2 oxides can slide on the <i>ab</i> plane. During the desodiation, the interlayer force changes and the interlayer slip will occur. Meanwhile, to reach the equilibrium state, the sodium ions and vacancies in the layer are rearranged to form multiple metastable states. Then in the region below 2.5&nbsp;V, Jahn-Teller distortion occurs due to the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction, which also can be attributed to the P2-P’2 phase transformation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0041_R_d32229211e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the redox peaks in the first few cycles are well overlapped after the activation process in the 1st cycle, indicating the high reversibility of the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202203802-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/adcbe06d-2d1e-4aaf-9d2c-6282c5ffe047/aenm202203802-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/adcbe06d-2d1e-4aaf-9d2c-6282c5ffe047/aenm202203802-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/084effbd-8384-4147-96b9-dcf3758348e2/aenm202203802-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/adcbe06d-2d1e-4aaf-9d2c-6282c5ffe047/aenm202203802-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202203802-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202203802" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of different samples. a) Cyclic voltammograms of NMO-0&nbsp;W and NMO-1&nbsp;W between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles of different electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;C for the second cycle (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;121&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). c) Cycling performance at 1&nbsp;C. d) Rate capability of different electrodes. e) The Nyquist plots, equivalent circuit and fitted curves of W-doped electrodes tested at 2.8&nbsp;V after 1 cycle. f) The relationship between <i>−Z″</i> and <i>ω<sup>−1/2</sup></i> at low frequency. g) Long-term cycling performance of NMO-1&nbsp;W at 5&nbsp;C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3b</a> exhibits the typical charge and discharge profiles of different electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;C (12.1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in the potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. The reversible specific capacities are 113.0, 178.8, 195.5, and 189.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for NMO-0&nbsp;W, NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, NMO-1&nbsp;W, and NMO-2&nbsp;W, respectively. All the W-doped samples show higher specific capacities than the undoped sample, indicating that the W-doping has a positive effect on the specific capacity improvement. The great increase in discharge capacity mainly comes from the 2.2–2.3&nbsp;V platforms, which can be attributed to the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox process. As the obtained samples are Na-deficient, the second charging capacity is much larger than that of the initial cycle for all the electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). It is worth noting that the reversible capacity of the sample is significantly improved with only 0.5%&nbsp;W doping, which is 59% higher than that of pristine NMO-0&nbsp;W. Impressively, the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode with a pure layered structure has the highest specific capacity and energy density among all the electrodes. Compared with the NMO-0&nbsp;W electrode, the reversible specific capacity of the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode is increased by 80&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the energy density is increased from 316&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> to 517&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. With further increasing of W concentration, the NMO-2&nbsp;W with Na<sub>2</sub>WO<sub>4</sub> impurity in the structure shows decreased capacity. In general, W<sup>6+</sup> without d electrons cannot interact with oxygen through the d orbital, thus orbital hybridization only occurs between oxygen and manganese.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0045_R_d32229211e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate the cycling performance of the electrodes at 1&nbsp;C. NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, NMO-1&nbsp;W, and NMO-2&nbsp;W show capacity retention of 90.9%, 85.0%, and 83.8% over 50 cycles, respectively. As per both the reversible capacity and cycling performance, NMO-1&nbsp;W is considered to be the best choice. The W-doped samples exhibit greatly enhanced capacity retention compared to the layered P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0042_R_d32229211e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Stor. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrating that W-doping is an efficient method to improve the structural stability to obtain boosted cycling performance of the Mn-based system. Even at a high current rate of 5&nbsp;C, the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode also delivers high capacity retention of 80% over 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). To show the structure stability of the electrode during the cycling, the XRD patterns of the electrodes before and after the cycling test are provided (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). It can be seen that the major peak intensity of the doped samples has no big difference, indicating that the crystalline is well sustained after 100 cycles at 5&nbsp;C. The rate capabilities of electrodes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3d</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode exhibits the best electrochemical performance with average discharge capacities of 194, 180, 154, 132, 110, and 75&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5&nbsp;C, respectively. The enhancement of rate performance could be ascribed to W doping with increased interlayer spacing, which could advance Na<sup>+</sup> transportation.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed to further investigate the effect of W-doping on the charge transfer and diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the EIS measurements of the cathodes by using a frequency range of 0.01&nbsp;Hz-100&nbsp;kHz at 2.8&nbsp;V. All the EIS profiles consist of one semicircle at the high-medium frequency and a sloping line at the low frequency. There is an intercept at <i>Z’</i> axis can be observed, which corresponds to electrolyte solution resistance and electric contact resistance (<i>R<sub>s</sub></i>). The semicircle is associated with charge-transfer resistance (<i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>) at the electrolyte/electrode interface. The sloping line at low frequency correlates to Warburg impedance (<i>Z<sub>w</sub></i>) related to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the active materials. The <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> of NMO-0.5&nbsp;W and NMO-1&nbsp;W is much lower than the NMO-2&nbsp;W, indicating a lower charge transfer impedance at the interface between electrode and electrolyte (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i>) can be calculated according to the following equation:

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Where <i>R</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>A, n</i>, <i>F</i>, and <i>C</i> are the gas constant, thermodynamic temperature, surface area of the electrode, number of electrons per reaction, Faraday constant, and molar concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the lattice, respectively. <i>σ</i> represents Warburg's coefficient, which can be calculated by the following equation:

                           </msup>

                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} -Z^{\prime}\ = \sigma {\omega }^{ - \frac{1}{2}} +2{C}_{d}{\sigma}^{2} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Where the <i>ω</i> is the angular frequency and the <i>σ</i> can be obtained by the profile of −<i>Z″</i> versus <i>ω<sup>−</sup><sup>1/2</sup></i> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0003">3f</a>. NMO-0.5&nbsp;W has the highest <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> due to the existence of part of unique 3D tunnel structures, while the NMO-1&nbsp;W has moderate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in tunnel structure is more facile than the layered structure. Besides, the impurity in NMO-2&nbsp;W may act as a barrier, resulting in the lowest <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> of NMO-2&nbsp;W (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To explore the structure evolution of NMO-1&nbsp;W in the process of charge and discharge, in situ XRD measurements were performed (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0004">4</a>). The corresponding contour map during the initial two cycles is exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0004">4a</a>. During the initial charge, the intensity of the (002) diffraction peak increases first and then decreases until it disappears, along with the gradual shift of the (002) diffraction peak to a lower angle. The Na-O attraction of the Na layers becomes weaker while the O-O repulsion of adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> layers increases with the continuous release of Na ions during the charging process, resulting in the enhancement of the interlayer space. In the meantime, when the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode is charged to 3.6&nbsp;V, a grand new diffraction peak appears, which is attributed to the OP4 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0042_R_d32229211e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Stor. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It shifts ≈1.28° to higher angles upon extraction of 0.065 Na<sup>+</sup> at a high charge potential within 3.6-4.0&nbsp;V, corresponding to the gliding of adjacent TM slabs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0046_R_d32229211e1252" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Commun.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then the peak of the OP4 phase gradually shifts back to lower angles, while the intensity becomes weaker and broader during the discharge process. At the same time, the (002) peak reversible appears, indicating that the structure is back to the original P2 Phase. With more insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the (002) peak shifts to a higher region while the intensity decreases, indicating a solid-solution reaction in 2.1-3&nbsp;V. With the increase of sodium content in the interlayer, the electrostatic repulsive between the oxygen atoms in interlayer decreases, resulting in a smaller interlayer spacing. Below 2.1&nbsp;V, the (002) peak splits into two peaks, which belong to the P’2 phase (<i>cmcm</i>) at ≈16.08°. The voltage region where P2 and P’2 coexist is the main region that leads to capacity decay, in which irreversible structural transition caused by the increase of Jahn-Teller active Mn<sup>3+</sup> would happen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0041_R_d32229211e1264" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Overall, the NMO-1&nbsp;W undergoes phase conversion from the OP4 phase into P2 and P′2 phase during discharge with total insertion of 0.714&nbsp;Na<sup>+</sup>, as schematically shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0004">4b,c</a>. The changes in cell parameters during the charge/discharge process were obtained from the refinement results of in situ XRD (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The volume change rate of the OP4 phase and P’2 phase relative to the P2 phase is 16.4% and 1.3%, respectively. Besides, the NMO-0&nbsp;W exhibits small structure and volume changes during the charge/discharge process (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202203802-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e98d25a2-d10f-4d5d-9f25-a6045ab4e6e1/aenm202203802-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e98d25a2-d10f-4d5d-9f25-a6045ab4e6e1/aenm202203802-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/61c203a1-aff2-4c52-8c0e-9b30b2f3b552/aenm202203802-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e98d25a2-d10f-4d5d-9f25-a6045ab4e6e1/aenm202203802-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202203802-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202203802" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns collected during the representative charge and discharge of the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode under a current rate of 0.1&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;121&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at a potential range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. b) Evolution of crystal structure, phase, and Na<sup>+</sup> content during the charge and discharge processes. c) The schematic crystal structures of the P2, OP4, and P’2 phases.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Water can be intercalated into the layered structures to solvate the sodium ions situated between the MO<sub>2</sub> layers. This phenomenon is widely encountered in P2-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TmO<sub>2</sub> electrodes, resulting in the degradation of the structure and electrochemical performance. As the H<sub>2</sub>O insertion can expand the interlayer distance of the Na<sup>+</sup> layers, the change of the (00<i>l</i>) peak position during H<sub>2</sub>O insertion will be easily distinguished by XRD. The XRD patterns of the NMO-1&nbsp;W exposed to moist air (NMO-1W-H) and the subsequently dried sample (NMO-1W-D) are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0005">5a</a>. A new diffraction peak ≈12.8° of NMO-1W-H can be indexed to the hydration phase, which is commonly reported as birnessite in Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TmO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0047_R_d32229211e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Mater.">47</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0048_R_d32229211e1336" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During interaction with a moist atmosphere, sodium ions are extracted from the bulk to form some amorphous Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>/NaHCO<sub>3</sub> particles on the surface. And then the H<sub>2</sub>O molecules move into the sodium layers, forming the hydration phase. The NMO-1W-H was dehydrated by respectively washing and drying to remove the NaCO<sub>3</sub>/NaHCO<sub>3</sub> impurities and water molecules to form the dehydration sample (NMO-1W-D).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0049_R_d32229211e1354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The hydration peak nearly disappears and the (002) peak reappears for the NMO-1W-D (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0005">5b</a>). Compared with pristine NMO-1&nbsp;W, the (002) peak of NMO-1W-D shifts to a lower angle, indicating a broader spacing of the layers. In addition, the major peak broadens and loses its intensity. This can be explained by the inhomogeneous broadening of the (002) plane with a reduced atomic ordering degree.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0046_R_d32229211e1363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Commun.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The morphological change of the hydration and dehydration samples are examined. After exposure, some nanoparticles can be observed on the surface and the sides of NMO-1W-H with severe delamination damages (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). However, the surface and sides of NMO-1W-D are smooth and clean, demonstrating that the impurities are removed completely (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0005">5e,f</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202203802-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1ae98e01-f887-4fe8-a81c-0af35b54fbd3/aenm202203802-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1ae98e01-f887-4fe8-a81c-0af35b54fbd3/aenm202203802-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f383b640-23ae-4a2a-a873-713051feb66f/aenm202203802-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1ae98e01-f887-4fe8-a81c-0af35b54fbd3/aenm202203802-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202203802-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202203802" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) XRD patterns of the NMO-1&nbsp;W, NMO-1W-H, and NMO-1W-D. SEM images of c,d) NMO-1W-H and e,f) NMO-1W-D.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>It is widely reported that the instability of transition metal oxides toward moisture leads to the great deterioration of electrochemical performance, which hinders the practical application of transition metal oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0024_R_d32229211e1396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Acc. Chem. Res.">24</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0050_R_d32229211e1396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0051_R_d32229211e1396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To investigate the effect of the hydration/dehydration process on the electrochemical properties of the NMO-1W-D, the half-cells based on the NMO-1W-D were assembled and evaluated. Interestingly, the dehydration NMO-1W-D electrode shows similar CV curves to the pristine NMO-1&nbsp;W (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0006">6a</a>). Encouragingly, the NMO-1W-D electrode also exhibits alike charge-discharge stages with an insignificant amount of capacity loss (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0006">6b</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), accompanying decent cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0006">6c</a>). These results indicate the enhanced structural stability of the P2-NMO-1&nbsp;W toward moist air and water treatment, as the layered P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is reported to show a severe capacity loss after water treatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0049_R_d32229211e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To investigate the general applicability, water-soluble binder carboxyl methyl cellulose (CMC) with low cost and nontoxicity is employed for electrode preparation. The electrochemical properties of different W-doped samples with the CMC binder (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202203802-fig-0006">6d–f</a>) are similar to the electrodes with the PVDF binder. Besides, NMO-1&nbsp;W still exhibits the best electrochemical performance with excellent cycle stability and rate performance among the W-doped samples. Specifically, the NMO-1&nbsp;W electrode demonstrates decent capacity retention of 93.2% after 50 cycles, and superior rate capability with average discharge capacities of 183.6, 173.9, 158, 142.3, 12.6, and 92.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5&nbsp;C, respectively. Besides, the charge-discharge curves of the W-doped electrodes using CMC are smoother than those of the electrodes with PVDF, and the multi-platform phase transition is alleviated. These results indicate the high adaptability of NMO-1&nbsp;W to the humidity atmosphere, which is indeed encouraging to promote the layer metal oxides for practical application.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202203802-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6353732d-d4f7-4e80-9379-1efa998ab906/aenm202203802-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6353732d-d4f7-4e80-9379-1efa998ab906/aenm202203802-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/626b4615-f932-4129-8e9c-718b8e0aee3b/aenm202203802-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6353732d-d4f7-4e80-9379-1efa998ab906/aenm202203802-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202203802-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202203802" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Water treatment on the electrochemical performance of W-doped samples. The electrochemical performance of NMO-1&nbsp;W and NMO-1W-D electrodes, a) CV curves between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, b) galvanostatic charge-discharge profile at 0.1&nbsp;C rate (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;121&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and c) cycling performance at 1&nbsp;C. The electrochemical performance of W-doped samples with the CMC binder, d) galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles at 0.1&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;121&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), e) cycling performance at 1&nbsp;C, and f) rate capability.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In summary, trace W-doping induced tunnel-to-layer transition of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is reported for the first time. The W-doping not only improves the reversible capacity but also prolongs the cycling lifespan. The NMO-1&nbsp;W exhibits a high reversible capacity of 195.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a remarkable energy density of 517&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C, along with high capacity retention of 80% after 200 cycles. In situ XRD characterizations reveal that NMO-1&nbsp;W undergoes phase conversion from the OP4 phase into P2 and P′2 phase during discharge with total insertion of 0.714 Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the W-doping endows the samples with improved stability toward water treatment. The W-doped samples maintain decent electrochemical performance after being treated in a severe humidity atmosphere and even using the water-based CMC binder for electrode preparation. This work provides an inspiring example that trace doping can effectively introduce tunnel-to-layered structure transformation to achieve highly stable structure as well as highly reversible sodium storage reactions.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202203802-sec-0030-title">3 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0040">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0050">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0060">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202203802-sec-0060-title"> Preparation of Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>W<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub></h4>
                  
                  <p>A series of Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>1-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>W<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> samples (Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.995</sub>W<sub>0.005</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>W<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.98</sub>W<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, denoted as NMO-0&nbsp;W, NMO-0.5&nbsp;W, NMO-1&nbsp;W, NMO-2&nbsp;W) were prepared by a simple solid-state reaction. Typically, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, MnCO<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>WO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O from Sigma-Aldrich were used as raw materials. And 5% excess Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was added to compensate for the evaporation of sodium at high temperatures. These raw materials were mixed through ball milling with 5&nbsp;mL of acetone for 12&nbsp;h. Then the mixture precursor was placed in a vacuum oven at 100&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h to completely remove the solvent. Afterward, the agglomerated material was thoroughly ground by hand in a mortar and annealed at 950&nbsp;°C (850&nbsp;°C is the optimum sintering temperature for NMO-0&nbsp;W) for 15&nbsp;h with a heating rate of 2 °C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>. There was no special atmosphere protection throughout the sintering process.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202203802-sec-0070-title"> Preparation of Hydration and Dehydration Sodium Oxides</h4>
                  
                  <p>The above-synthesized NMO-1&nbsp;W sample was placed in a centrifugal tube, which was put in a sealed beaker containing 20&nbsp;mL of distilled water and it was kept at room temperature for 2 days to obtain the hydration sample (NMO-1W-H). To prepare the dehydration samples (NMO-1W-D), 0.5 gram of NMO-1W-H was added in 20&nbsp;mL of deionized water and stirred for 10&nbsp;min at room temperature, then centrifuged and washed with ethanol, followed by drying at 120 °C for 6&nbsp;h and dehydration at 160 °C for 24&nbsp;h in a vacuum atmosphere. The final samples were stored in an Ar-filled glove box.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202203802-sec-0080-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The contents of metal elements in obtained samples were determined by inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES, IRIS Intrepid II XSP, USA). The crystal structures of as-prepared samples were investigated by X-ray diffractometer (XRD, Rigaku, SmartLab SE) with Cu K<i>α</i> radiation with a step size of 0.01° from 15°-70° and analyzed via fullprof software. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, ZEISS merlin Compact, Germany) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-2100FEF, Japan) were used to observe the morphology of the samples. The Energy-dispersive X-ray (EDS) spectrum mapping was carried out on an Oxford detector (X-MaxN, Oxford Instruments) attached to the SEM. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) result was obtained by a spectrometer (ESCA-LAB 250 Xi spectrometer, England). The plate-like particle was thinned by the Focused Ion Beam (Tescan AMBER, Czech). The ABF-STEM and HADDF images were obtained by Spherical aberration electron microscopy (JEM-NEOARM, JEOL, Japan). HRTEM was used to obtain the result of EDX-point (JEM-2100plus, JEOL, Japan) and EDX mapping (JEM-2100F, JEOL, Japan).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202203802-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Samples were mixed with carbon black (Acetylene black) and binder (polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) dissolved in N-methylpyrrolidone or carboxymethylcellulose sodium (CMC) dissolved in water) in a weight ratio of 80:10:10. The mixture slurry was mixed through a Mini-mill (Fritsch, pulverisette23, Germany) for 18&nbsp;min. Then the slurry was cast on an aluminum foil with 100&nbsp;um and dried at 100&nbsp;°C in an oven for 2&nbsp;h. The loading mass of the electrode was ≈2.0&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with a negative electrode of Na metal as both counter and reference electrodes. The electrolyte with 1&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC, 1:1 by vol.) and 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was used. And a polypropylene microporous film (Celgard 2300) was used as the separator. Cyclic voltammograms (CV) were carried out at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> using an electrochemical working station (Autolab PGSTAT128N). The electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) measurement was investigated on AutoLab (Metrohm Co., Ltd., Switzerland) over a frequency range of 100&nbsp;kHz to 10&nbsp;mHz with an amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV. The galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements were employed in the Neware test system (Neware Technology Limited).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202203802-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202203802-sec-0100-title"> Computational Method</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">All density functional theory (DFT) calculations were carried out in Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0052_R_d32229211e1606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Phys. Rev. B">52</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0053_R_d32229211e1606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Phys. Rev. B">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the projector augment wave (PAW) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0054_R_d32229211e1611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Phys. Rev. B">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The self-interaction energy was approximated with generalized gradient approximation (GGA)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0055_R_d32229211e1616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Phys. Rev. B">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) exchange functional.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0056_R_d32229211e1622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the structure optimizing process, the cutoff energy of plane-wave basis set and Monkhorst-Pack k-points meshes were set as 500&nbsp;eV and 3&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;×&nbsp;1. And the self-consistent convergence accuracy and convergence criterion for the force between atoms were set as 1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV&nbsp;cell<sup>−1</sup> and 1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−2</sup>&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, Hubbard correction U method was employed to describe the interactions between d electrons accurately. For Mn and W, the U parameters were set as 3.9&nbsp;eV<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0034_R_d32229211e1636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0057_R_d32229211e1636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Catal.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 4.0&nbsp;eV<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202203802-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202203802-bib-0058_R_d32229211e1641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Mater. Chem. A">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively, based on the previous researches. The P2 and tunnel structures were calculated in 3a&nbsp;×&nbsp;3b&nbsp;×&nbsp;3c supercell and a&nbsp;×&nbsp;b&nbsp;×&nbsp;3c supercell, respectively. Both cells contain 24&nbsp;Na, 53&nbsp;Mn, 1&nbsp;W, and 108&nbsp;O atoms to simulate Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.99</sub>W<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with two configurations. And to explore the occupation sites of Na and the substitution site of W, a series of structures had been optimized and cell with the lowest energy was adopted to calculate the formation energy with the following formula:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202203802-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/19a32d1b-48bd-4fe7-9031-f79b89d15df4/aenm202203802-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202203802:aenm202203802-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202203802-math-0003.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>form</mi>
                                          <mi>ation</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>cell</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <mn>24</mn>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>Na</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <mn>53</mn>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>Mn</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>W</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <mn>108</mn>

                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>O</mi>

                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {\ {E}_{\textit{form}\textit{ation}}={E}_{\textit{cell}}\ -24{E}_{\textit{Na}}-53{E}_{\textit{Mn}}-{E}_{W}-108{E}_{O}} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

      <section class="article-section article-section__references" id="article-references-section-1">
